State v. Stein (in Re Stein)


No. 20813-0-II (consolidated), No. 21767-8-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


94 Wn. App. 616; 972 P.2d 505; 1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 338



February 26, 1999, Filed


NOTICE:  PUBLISHED IN PART


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Petition for Review Granted July 11, 2000, Reported at: 2000 Wn. LEXIS 414.
Reconsideration denied by, 05/10/1999
Review pending at State v. Stein, 1999 Wash. LEXIS 783 (Wash., Nov. 2, 1999)
Review granted by State v. Stein, 1999 Wash. LEXIS 805 (Wash., Nov. 4, 1999)
Affirmed by, Remanded by State v. Stein, 144 Wn.2d 236, 27 P.3d 184, 2001 Wash. LEXIS 472 (2001)
Subsequent appeal at, Petition denied by State v. Stein, 140 Wn. App. 43, 165 P.3d 16, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 2363 (2007)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Stein v. Frakes, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 32327 (W.D. Wash., Apr. 15, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision of the Superior Court of Clark County (Washington) that convicted him of attempted murder and burglary on the basis of vicarious liability for the actions of others. Defendant claimed that the trial court's jury instructions were defective because they improperly allowed the jury to hold defendant vicariously liable without finding him guilty of all elements of accomplice liability.


OVERVIEW: In Washington state court defendant was acquitted of conspiracy but convicted of attempted murder and burglary on the basis of vicarious liability for the actions of others. He appealed his convictions, claiming that the trial court erred in giving Pinkerton instructions on conspiracy because the Pinkerton doctrine was not the law in Washington. Defendant also claimed that even if the Pinkerton doctrine was the law, the trial court's instructions were defective because they improperly allowed the jury to hold defendant vicariously liable without finding him guilty of all elements of accomplice liability. The appellate court reversed defendant's convictions. It held that the trial court's instructions were fatally defective because they did not advise the jury that it could not find defendant guilty for the substantive acts of co-conspirators unless it also found him guilty of conspiracy. Failure to instruct on an element of an offense was reversible error, so defendant was entitled to reversal of his convictions. Because the instructions were fatally defective, the court declined to rule on whether the Pinkerton doctrine was the law in Washington.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed defendant's convictions for attempted murder and burglary on the basis of vicarious liability for the actions of others. The appellate court explained that defendant was entitled to reversal because the trial court issued jury instructions that improperly allowed the jury to convict defendant for the substantive crimes of his co-conspirators without convicting him for conspiracy.

Kahuna Land Co. v. Spokane County


No. 17114-1-III


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION THREE, PANEL FIVE


94 Wn. App. 836; 974 P.2d 1249; 1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 320



February 25, 1999, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Publication Status of this Document has been Changed by the Court from Unpublished to Publication March 23, 1999.
Reported in Table Case Format at: 94 Wn. App. 1016.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowner challenged a decision from Superior Court of Spokane County (Washington), affirming a zoning board decision requiring it to build a road and connect the sewer through the adjoining land owned by the federal government as a condition of approval of its development plan.


OVERVIEW: Appellant land owner sought approval of its development plan for a parcel of land. Its application was approved with the condition that it build an access road and connect the sewer line through the adjacent federal land. The trial court affirmed the conditions. Appellant challenged the decision arguing the conditions were unconstitutional, an unlawful taking, and violative of substantive due process, thereby arbitrary and capricious. In affirming the decision, the court found that the property could be developed and had economic viability, even though not to the extent appellant desired. Thus, there was no unconstitutional taking of the land. Since the required conditions served solely to benefit appellant, his substantive due process rights were not violated. The conditions served the legitimate public purpose of insuring adequate access to the property and sanitary disposal of waste. Requiring appellant to connect the road and sewer to existing services was reasonably necessary and not unduly oppressive.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision requiring appellant to construct a road and sewer line through the adjacent federal property as part of the approval of his development plan as the conditions did not constitute an unconstitutional taking of the land, were reasonably necessary, and not unduly oppressive.

Anderson v. Wilson

OVERVIEW: Petitioners charged respondents with the offenses of driving or being in actual physical possession of a motor vehicle while under the influence of intoxicating liquor or drugs (DUI). Respondents moved for a dismissal on the ground that the statutes under which they were charged were unconstitutional. The lower court held that prosecutions under Wash. Rev. On appeal, the court affirmed holding that the state's equal protection/privileges and immunities clause was more protective than the federal equal protection/privileges and immunities clause and as such interpretation of the state provision meant more protection for the citizens of Washington.

Lytle v. Dep't of Licensing


No. 16910-3-III


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION THREE


94 Wn. App. 357; 971 P.2d 969; 1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 342



February 25, 1999, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant driver sought judicial review of the administrative suspension of his driver's license, pursuant to the implied consent law, Wash. Rev. Code § 46.20.308, for his alleged refusal to take a breath alcohol test upon his arrest for driving under the influence.


OVERVIEW: Appellant driver was pulled over, and when he failed field sobriety tests, he was arrested for driving under the influence. At the station house, appellant refused to sign an implied consent acknowledgement and refused to submit to a breath test. The arresting state troopers then submitted reports to respondent department regarding appellant's refusal. When his driver's license was administratively suspended under the implied consent law, Wash. Rev. Code § 46.20.308, appellant requested a hearing and subpoenaed the troopers. Two troopers did not appear, so the hearing was continued and the subpoenas reissued. At the second hearing, none of the troopers appeared. Appellant's motion to dismiss was denied, and based solely on the trooper's written reports, as permitted by § 46.20.308(8), the suspension was upheld. Appellant then sought judicial review. In reversing, the court ruled that revocation of a driver's license was a property interest that was subject to due process, including the right to confront witnesses. Appellant's due process rights were violated by his inability to obtain the attendance of the troopers and the denial of an opportunity to cross-examine them.


OUTCOME: The administrative suspension of appellant's driver's license was reversed because his procedural due process rights were denied by respondent department's reliance on police reports, as permitted by statute, when he was frustrated in his attempts to compel the attendance of the state troopers who arrested him and thus was denied the opportunity to confront and cross-examine the evidence used against him.

 

1298 of 2268 DOCUMENTS


Anderson v. Wilson


No. 15519-6-III


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION THREE, PANEL FOUR


1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 343



February 25, 1999, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported in Table Case Format at: 94 Wn. App. 1015.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Spokane County. Docket No: 95-2-02437-2. Date filed: 00/00/0000. Judge signing: Hon. Robert Austin.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed from the Superior Court, Spokane County, (Washington), which held former Wash. Rev. Code §§ 46.61.5051-.5053 defined new crimes, separate from the drug and alcohol offenses defined in §§ 46.61.502 and .504, which were not subject to minimum penalties because they required proof of additional elements, and, as the prosecutor's charging discretion was limited, were saved from alleged constitutional violations.


OVERVIEW: The lower court found no constitutional violations in the application of former Wash. Rev. Code §§ 46.61.5051-.5053 to defendants because proof of additional elements of driving under the influence of drugs or alcohol other than those of sections 46.61.502 and .504 were required and because defendant who pled guilty or was found guilty after trial could receive exactly the same sentence. The court affirmed, holding that permitting but not requiring a sentencing court to suspend all or part of the otherwise mandatory 90-day license suspension in return for a guilty plea did not place an unconstitutional burden on the exercise of defendant's constitutional right to a jury trial. Those who pled not guilty and those who pled guilty remained subject to the same possible maximum penalty but a proper degree of leniency could be obtained in return for a guilty plea. Equal protection under Wash. Const. art. I, § 12 was not violated because proof of additional elements to those required under sections 46.502 and .504 limited the prosecutor's charging discretion under former sections 46.61.5051-.5053. Only statutes with identical elements but harsher penalties violated the state constitution.


OUTCOME: The decision of the lower court upholding the constitutionality of statutes providing harsher penalties for driving while under the influence of drugs or alcohol and lesser penalties for a guilty plea thereto was affirmed because the requirement of additional elements limited the prosecutor's charging authority and the maximum possible sentence could be received for either a conviction or a plea.

 

1299 of 2268 DOCUMENTS


State v. Hoggatt


No. 21560-8-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 288



February 19, 1999, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported in Table Case Format at: 94 Wn. App. 1010.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Cowlitz County. Docket No: 95-1-00539-7. Date filed: 01/15/97. Judge signing: Hon. Don L. McCulloch.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Cowlitz County (Washington), entered upon a jury verdict, convicting him of first degree burglary, Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.52.020, and imposing a life sentence as a persistent offender under the "three strikes" law, Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.120(4).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was implicated in a burglary, and his cohabiting girlfriend consented to a search of their home, during which deputies found a pellet gun that was taken in the burglary. A deputy advised him of his Miranda rights, questioned him, and then arrested him. He was taken to the station house, where he gave a statement without being re-advised of his rights. Defendant's motion to suppress the statement was denied, and he was convicted of first degree burglary, Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.52.020. Evidence was presented at sentencing that he had six prior felony convictions, including robbery and second degree assault. Pursuant to the "three strikes" persistent offender law, Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.120(4), he was sentenced to life imprisonment. Defendant appealed. In affirming, the court ruled that deputies were not required to repeat the Miranda warnings that were given before defendant's arrest. The search was valid due to consent, and defendant failed to show manifest error in admitting the pellet gun. Also, § 9.94A.120(4) was not retroactive and his sentence was not cruel or excessive, especially since first degree burglary was already punishable by up to life in prison.


OUTCOME: The conviction and sentence were affirmed because the warrantless search of his mobile home was valid due to the consent of his live-in girlfriend; defendant was given Miranda warnings prior to his arrest, and his later custodial statements were admissible without the warnings being repeated; the three-strikes law was not impermissibly retroactive; and the life sentence was not cruel or excessive punishment.

State v. Kellogg


No. 41220-5-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 255



February 16, 1999, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported in Table Case Format at: 94 Wn. App. 1003.


PRIOR HISTORY: Date first document (petition, etc) was filed in Court of Appeals: 09/02/1997.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the dismissal in the King County Superior Court (Washington) of his motion to set aside his guilty plea for second degree burglary on grounds it was not made voluntarily and knowingly.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed the dismissal of his motion to set aside his guilty plea for second degree burglary on grounds it was not made voluntarily and knowingly. The court affirmed, holding that under Wash Rev. Code § 10.73.090, a defendant found guilty of a crime was required to file a postconviction motion or petition for collateral relief within one year after the conviction became final, and defendant's motion was made over five years after he pleaded guilty. Defendant also alleged the statute under which he was convicted was unconstitutional. Therefore, he asserted that the exception to the one-year limitation found in Wash. Rev. Code § 10.73.100(2) applied and his claim was not barred. The court held that defendant failed to carry his burden of proof in showing the statute in question was unconstitutional for purposes of Wash. Rev. Code § 10.73.100 (2), and the exception therefore did not apply.


OUTCOME: The denial of defendant's motion to set aside his guilty plea was affirmed because the motion was not made within the one-year time limit after the conviction became final, and there was no applicable exception to that rule.

 

1301 of 2268 DOCUMENTS


Larsen v. Town of Colton


No. 17059-4-III


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION THREE


94 Wn. App. 383; 973 P.2d 1066; 1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 317



February 16, 1999, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Publication Status of this Document has been Changed by the Court from Unpublished to Published March 9, 1999.
Reported in Table Case Format at: 94 Wn. App. 1002.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Whitman County (Washington), which had enjoined them from building a garage or shop on their Colton property on the ground that structure was not permitted by the town's zoning ordinance.


OVERVIEW: On review, appellant landowners contended that the Land Use Petition Act (LUPA), Wash. Rev. Code § 36.70C, provided the exclusive remedy to respondent neighbors' action and that the action was not filed within the applicable limitations period. The court held that respondents were not required to comply with LUPA because they did not have standing to pursue its remedies as provided for in Wash. Rev. Code § 36.70C.060, noting that the building inspector was not required to consider respondents' interests in determining whether to issue a building permit to appellants. The court further found that respondents' complaint for an injunction was timely, noting that respondents had initiated their lawsuit one week after they became specifically aware that the building permit had been issued, which was a reasonable period of time for limitations purposes. The court affirmed, finding that the local zoning ordinance properly prohibited the building of appellants' proposed structure where the structure was not "appurtenant" to the residence, as required under § 4(c) of the ordinance, because it was built on a lot adjoining to, but separate from, the house.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the injunction against appellant landowners where the local zoning ordinance prohibited the building of the proposed structure because it was not appurtenant to appellants' residence, given that it was on an adjoining lot to the home; respondent neighbors lacked standing to sue under the Land Use Petition Act because their interests were not considered in issuing appellants' building permit.

State v. Snook


No. 15270-7-III


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION THREE, PANEL EIGHT


1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 347



February 16, 1999, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported in Table Case Format at: 94 Wn. App. 1001.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Walla Walla County. Docket No: 68330. Date filed: 07/28/1977. Judge signing: Hon. James B. Mitchell.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Walla Walla County (Washington), which convicted him of the aggravated first degree murder of a prison inmate, committed while he was serving a life sentence for a previous prison murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought reversal of his murder conviction, claiming a 20-year delay in perfecting his appeal violated due process. He alleged he was denied a speedy trial; his right to appear and defend were infringed upon by courtroom security procedures including shackles; premeditation was not proved; he was not permitted to be present at all stages of the trial, and he received ineffective assistance of counsel. The court affirmed defendant's conviction for murder of a fellow inmate under Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.32.046 (1975). Defendant was not prejudiced by any delay in the appeal process, as there was no substantive merit to his appeal. The court concluded that the trial court did not err in denying a motion for speedy trial and granting a continuance under Wash. Super. Ct. Crim. R. 3.3(e)(3) (1976). The trial court did not abuse its discretion by declining defendant's offer to waive objection to a security order. Defendant failed to show he was denied due process or effective assistance of counsel. In short, the evidence showed that defendant committed premeditated murder, and defendant failed to show any prejudice in the conduct of the case warranting reversal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for murder, as premeditation was established, and he failed to show any due process violation or other prejudice in the conduct of the case.

 

1303 of 2268 DOCUMENTS


State v. Matthews


No. 22875-1-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 240



February 12, 1999, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported in Table Case Format at: 93 Wn. App. 1090.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Grays Harbor County. Docket No: 97-1-00165-2. Date filed: 01/26/1998. Judge signing: Hon. Gordon L. Godfrey.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Grays Harbor County (Washington), denying appellant's suppression motion, granting the prosecution's motion to prevent appellant's use of certain defenses, and entering judgment of conviction of manufacture and possession of marijuana in violation of the Uniform Controlled Substances Act, Wash. Rev. Code § 69.50.401(a)(1).


OVERVIEW: Appellant, convicted of manufacturing marijuana, was under supervision. After receiving information suggesting that appellant was distributing marijuana, the police searched and found marijuana plants. The police obtained a warrant and seized the evidence. Appellant was charged with marijuana manufacture and possession under the Uniform Controlled Substances Act (the Act), Wash. Rev. Code §§ 69.50.401(a)(1) and 69.50.401(d). The trial court denied appellant's suppression motion and granted the prosecution's motion in limine to prevent appellant's religious and medical defenses. The trial court convicted appellant. On appeal, the court affirmed. The suppression motion was properly denied because appellant was subject to warrantless search as a condition of supervision, substantial evidence supported the finding that the police's entry was not forced, and the police reasonably suspected criminal behavior. The Act was constitutional. The trial court properly disallowed appellant from presenting a religious use defense because any such use yielded to the state's interest in marijuana regulation; appellant did not present medical evidence to prove necessity of use.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction; the denial of the motion to suppress was not an abuse of discretion because the warrantless search was proper as a condition of supervision and was supported by reasonable belief that appellant was distributing marijuana; the statute was constitutional; appellant's religious use defense was forbidden, and appellant did not submit evidence in support of his medical necessity defense.

 

1304 of 2268 DOCUMENTS


Malpica v. Mary M. Knight Sch. Dist. # 311


No. 23397-5-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 210



February 5, 1999, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported in Table Case Format at: 93 Wn. App. 1084.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Mason County. Docket No: 97-2-00670-1. Date filed: 12/08/1997. Judge signing: Hon. Toni A. Sheldon.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant teacher challenged a ruling from the Superior Court of Mason County (Washington), which granted respondent school district's motion to dismiss her claim for withheld pay for failure to comply with the filing requirements of Wash. Rev. Code § 28A.645.010.


OVERVIEW: Appellant teacher sued respondent school district for refusing to pay her for 13 days' work, during which appellant taught without a valid certificate as required by her contract. The trial court dismissed her claim for failure to meet the filing requirements of Wash. Rev. Code Ann. § 28A.645.010, holding it lacked subject matter jurisdiction. Appellant contended her suit was not subject to the statute, and, alternatively, its 30-day statute of limitations violated the equal protection clause. The court affirmed, holding that the statute applied to appellant's breach of contract claim, and its exceptions did not apply. Respondent simply refused to pay appellant for 13 days of work, which was analogous to reduction of pension benefits. The plain language of the statute did not require a formal written decision, and appellant was personally notified of respondent's plan to recoup the money paid in error. As appellant's equal protection claims were not based on a suspect classification, the rational basis test applied. The statute did not limit or bar a teacher's right to file suit, nor did it inflict a procedural disadvantage on district employees not required of non-employees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of appellant teacher's claim for withheld pay because she failed to meet the statutory filing requirements applicable to her breach of contract claim, and her equal protection claims failed under rational basis review.

 

1305 of 2268 DOCUMENTS


Wash. Citizen Action v. Ins. Comm'r


No. 23053-4-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


94 Wn. App. 64; 971 P.2d 527; 1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 211



February 5, 1999, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant citizen group challenged a decision from the Superior Court of Thurston County (Washington) denying it access to documents concerning the proposed acquisition of a health plan by a health cooperative under the Public Disclosure Act, Wash. Rev. Code § 42.17.


OVERVIEW: Appellant citizen group sought access to documents, filed with the Insurance Commissioner, of the proposed acquisition of a health plan by a health cooperative. After the request was denied, appellant sued to compel disclosure. The trial court granted summary judgment dismissing the complaint. On appeal, appellant contended that Wash. Rev. Code § 48.13.220(4)(g), preventing disclosure of documents filed with the commissioner, was unconstitutional. The court found that the statute was passed by a two-thirds vote, allowing it to amend the Public Disclosure Act (PDA) which had been enacted five months before. The statute was complete and did not require reference to other laws to make its meaning clear. There was no violation of the requirement that the bill not embrace more than one subject and that it be expressed in the title. Thus, the statute was constitutional. Under the PDA, public disclosure was required, except when exempt by another statute. There was no conflict requiring PDA be given priority. The court also found that its review was controlled by the Administrative Procedure Act and affirmed the decision denying disclosure.


OUTCOME: The court held that the statute denying public disclosure of documents supplied to the Insurance Commission was constitutional and that it controlled appellant's request for release of information. Further, the Administrative Procedure Act appeals procedure applied to the dispute.

 

1306 of 2268 DOCUMENTS


State v. Hollis


NO. 40956-5-I, NO. 41052-1-I (Linked Cases)


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


93 Wn. App. 804; 970 P.2d 813; 1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 179



February 1, 1999, Filed


NOTICE:   [***1] PUBLISHED IN PART.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Review Denied June 1, 1999, Reported at: 137 Wn.2d 1038, 980 P.2d 1285, 1999 Wash. LEXIS 422.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed from the Superior Court of King County (Washington), which convicted them of delivery of cocaine and involving a minor in a drug transaction; the state cross-appealed the sentence. Defendants contended that Wash. Rev. Code § 69.50.401(f), which defines the crime of involving a minor in a drug transaction, was unconstitutionally vague as applied.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged from separate incidents with delivery of cocaine within 1,000 feet of a school bus stop and also with involving a minor in a drug transaction in violation of Wash. Rev. Code § 69.50.401(f). In neither case was the minor culpable in the transaction; the minor did not handle the drugs or the money. In one case the minor was merely with the defendant during the sale. In the other case, defendant asked the minor to vouch for and to pat down the policeman posing as the buyer of the drugs. Defendants appealed their convictions, arguing that the statute was unconstitutionally vague as applied to them because it prohibited involving a minor in the transaction "in any manner." The court affirmed the convictions applying the ordinary meaning of the word "involve" and said that an ordinary person would understand that defendants' actions were proscribed by the statute. The court also held that involving a minor in a drug transaction was a separate offense from the transaction itself with differing elements, so conviction under both statutes did not violate the Double Jeopardy clause and both crimes were to be counted for the purposes of sentencing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' convictions because defendants' actions were prohibited by the ordinary meaning of the statute prohibiting involving minors in a drug transaction. The court reversed one conviction for lack of evidence and reversed one defendant's sentence because the crimes of selling drugs and involving a minor in the sale of drugs were separate offenses for the purpose of calculating that defendant's offense level.

State v. Johnson


No. 22792-4-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 154



January 29, 1999, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Clark County (Washington), entering judgment of conviction of unlawful possession of a firearm in the first degree and revoking appellant's driver's license under Wash. Rev. Code § 46.20.285(4).


OVERVIEW: A victim reported that he had been robbed at gunpoint by three black men, one of whom had braided hair, who were traveling in a metallic blue two-door car. A police officer heard the report and, following a car and occupants of matching descriptions, turned on his emergency lights. The driver drove the car into a dark alley, and the driver opened the door but crouched in the driver's seat. He was verbally abusive but eventually complied with police demands. The police handcuffed the three occupants, one of whom was appellant. The victim identified the three men. The police saw ammunition in the car and searched, seizing a handgun. The court denied appellant's motion to suppress and convicted appellant of unlawful possession of a firearm in the first degree, revoking his driver's license under Wash. Rev. Code § 46.20.285(4). On appeal, the court affirmed. The trial court's denial of the suppression motion was not in error because, under the totality of the circumstances, the police made a valid Terry stop and the search of the car was justifiable for safety concerns. § 46.20.285(4) was not unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction; the denial of appellant's motion to suppress was proper because the circumstances justified the Terry stop and search of the car; the statute requiring the revocation of appellant's driver's license was constitutional.

State v. Hull


No. 22039-3-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 158



January 29, 1999, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported in Table Case Format at: 93 Wn. App. 1073.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Clark County. Docket No: 97-1-00093-9. Date filed: 05/07/1997. Judge signing: Hon. Robert L. Harris.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the imposition of an exceptional sentence by the Superior Court of Clark County (Washington) after his conviction for second-degree murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant beat and stabbed his roommate to death, breaking his knees with an axe handle. He argued that his exceptional sentence for his second-degree murder conviction was improper because the sentencing court improperly relied upon deliberate cruelty as the single aggravating factor upon which it based the sentence. The reviewing court upheld the sentence. Deliberate cruelty occurs when the defendant's conduct in committing the offense includes gratuitous violence or other conduct that inflicts physical, psychological, or emotional pain as an end itself. Here, defendant inflicted numerous gratuitous blows over an extended and continuing period. Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.120(2), was not unconstitutionally vague and permitted an exceptional sentence based upon a single substantial and compelling aggravating factor. The statute did not violate his right to equal protection.


OUTCOME: The imposition of an exceptional sentence after defendant's conviction for second-degree murder was affirmed because he inflicted gratuitous pain upon his victim, which constituted deliberate cruelty.

Mitchem v. Morgan


No. 20994-2-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 163



January 29, 1999, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Review Denied June 1, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Wash. LEXIS 423.
Reported in Table Case Format at: 93 Wn. App. 1073.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Cowlitz County. Docket No: 93-2-01246-3. Date filed: 06/27/1996. Judge signing: Hon. Randolph Furman.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant physician sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Cowlitz County (Washington), entering judgment for respondent in respondent's medical negligence action.


OVERVIEW: Respondent suffered from a deformed foot, and appellant physician operated on the toe in an attempt to straighten and stabilize the joints. The foot worsened, and the toes required amputation. Respondent brought a medical malpractice action against appellant. Respondent's expert witnesses testified that appellant's actions did not meet the standard of care. The trial court entered judgment for respondent with a 23 percent reduction due to her own negligence. On appeal, the court affirmed. The opinion of respondent's expert witness that appellant was not qualified to perform the surgery was both relevant and supported by the physician's training and experience. An opinion that appellant erred by not lengthening a heel cord, if admitted in error, likely had no effect upon the verdict. The trial court's refusal to direct a verdict due to the reasonable minority opinion doctrine was proper because appellant did not demonstrate that his procedures were within the standard of care. Appellant's cross-examination of respondent was effective despite numerous objection interruptions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment for respondent; the expert opinions of respondent's witnesses were proper as the experts testified from their experience; appellant failed to show that a directed verdict due to the reasonable minority opinion doctrine was warranted; appellant's right to cross-examination of respondent was not abridged by numerous objections and court rulings.

Fusato v. WIAA


No. 17526-0-III


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION THREE, PANEL THREE


93 Wn. App. 762; 970 P.2d 774; 1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 147



January 28, 1999, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state interscholastic association sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Stevens County (Washington), which reversed the administrative decision against respondent foreign student and found that appellant's rules regarding student participation in varsity sports violated U.S. Cons. amend. XIV's Equal Protection Clause.


OVERVIEW: The court initially noted that even though the primary conflict was technically moot because respondent foreign student had returned to her country, discussion on appeal was warranted because the core issue was likely to recur and involved a matter of substantial public interest, that when resolved, would provide future guidance to the officers of appellant state interscholastic association. The court then held that it was not error to apply the strict scrutiny test when deciding whether appellant's residence and transfer rules violated respondent's equal protection rights, noting that respondent established that the rules discriminatorily impacted on her as a member of a suspect class based on national origin. The court further found that appellant failed to show that a compelling state interest was served by the challenged rules, noting that appellant made no effort to demonstrate that the least restrictive regulatory means were used to accomplish the stated purposes of the rules. The court affirmed, finding that it was not error to take judicial notice of the fact that typical foreign exchange students move to the United States without their family units.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision to reverse the administrative judgment against respondent foreign student, holding that appellant state interscholastic association's sports eligibility rules violated respondent's equal protection rights under the strict scrutiny test; the rules' discriminatorily impacted respondent based on her membership in a suspect class and appellant failed to show a compelling state interest served by the challenged rules.

State v. Lee (In re Lee)

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of King County. Docket No: 96-1-01011-8. Date filed: 03/28/97. Judge signing: Hon. Stephen G. Scott.



DISPOSITION: Lee's sentence affirmed and her personal restraint petition dismissed..


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed from her 1997 sentence by the Superior Court of King County (Washington) and filed a personal restraint petition to collaterally attack her 1995 convictions, which were considered in imposing the sentence.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner filed a direct appeal and personal restraint petition contending that the sentencing court improperly used two 1995 convictions for unlawful issuance of checks or drafts to calculate her offender score for her current unlawful issuance of checks conviction. The court affirmed her sentence and dismissed her personal restraint petition. Because petitioner's two 1995 convictions were not constitutionally invalid on their faces, the sentencing court properly used them to calculate her the offender score for her current conviction, notwithstanding petitioner's extrinsic evidence that her out-of-state attorney in those 1995 cases neither had court permission to appear pro hac vice nor associated himself with local counsel. Because petitioner filed her personal restraint petition attacking her 1995 convictions more than one year after they became final, and did not assert that any of the Wash. Rev. Code § 10.73.100 exceptions applied, her personal restraint petition was untimely under § 10.73.090.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed petitioner's sentence because the sentencing court properly used her convictions to calculate her offender score for her current conviction and dismissed her personal restraint petition because it was untimely.

Carl Constr. v. City of Tacoma


No. 20073-2-II, Consolidated with 21382-6-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 116



January 22, 1999, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported in Table Case Format at: 93 Wn. App. 1066.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Pierce County. Docket No: 91-2-06470-4. Date filed: 10/27/95. Judge signing: Hon. Waldo F. Stone.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff licensees appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Pierce County (Washington), which upheld defendant city's revocation and denial of home occupation permits and business licenses based on construction business equipment stored in plaintiff's backyard.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff licensees sued defendant city in a dispute over a number of actions pertaining to denial of an application or revocation of a home business permit. Plaintiffs operated a home-based contracting business on residential property in a Tacoma neighborhood. Defendant found that plaintiffs violated city ordinances by keeping commercial equipment on their property. Plaintiffs sued defendant, claiming they had expanded their business in detrimental reliance on a permit and were entitled to damages. Thus, plaintiffs' claim to violation of due process rights was without merit. Defendant was entitled to enforce its valid zoning laws.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the ruling upholding defendant city's determination that plaintiff licensees were not entitled to a home occupation permit due to violation of ordinances. Plaintiff's were keeping commercial equipment at their residence in violation of regulations prohibiting vehicles used in connection with home businesses from being maintained at a residential location.

State v. Escobar


No. 16786-1-III


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION THREE, PANEL FOUR


1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 100



January 21, 1999, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Review Denied June 1, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Wash. LEXIS 372.
Reported in Table Case Format at: 93 Wn. App. 1066.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Yakima County. Docket No: 95-1-01823-3. Date filed: 06/24/97. Judge signing: Hon. James P. Hutton.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Yakima County (Washington), which convicted him of second degree manslaughter.


OVERVIEW: Appellant's neighbor shot and killed appellant's chicken that had wandered onto the neighbor's property. Appellant entered the neighbor's yard and an altercation ensued. When the neighbor stopped poking appellant with a shotgun, appellant shot and killed the neighbor with a gun he had concealed in his pants pocket. Appellant was convicted of second degree manslaughter. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that appellant was not denied the opportunity to argue his theory of the case based upon the trial court's refusal to give certain proposed instructions. The court properly refused the proposed supplemental instructions regarding trespass, because appellant was not charged with trespass and had no right to kill the neighbor even if he had the right to enter his property. The court also properly refused the instruction on use of a deadly weapon to eject a nonviolent trespasser, because appellant was not a nonviolent trespasser by virtue of his concealed weapon and his physical confrontation with his neighbor. The court properly refused the proposed roaming animal instructions because appellant cited no authority for the use of deadly force to recover an animal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's second degree manslaughter conviction because the trial court did not abuse its discretion and appellant was able to argue his theory of defense with the jury instructions that had been given by the trial court.

Truancy of Perkins


No. 41166-7-I, Consolidated with case No. 41560-3-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


93 Wn. App. 590; 969 P.2d 1101; 1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 78



January 19, 1999, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Snohomish County (Washington), which found they had no right to appointed counsel during their truancy hearing and found them in contempt of a court order.


OVERVIEW: Appellants were students that had missed nearly every day of school. The school filed a truancy petition against them. The trial court entered an order requiring appellants to attend school without unexcused absences. When they failed to return to school, the school filed a contempt motion. The trial court found them in contempt and sentenced them to detention. On appeal, appellants raised the issue of the right to appointed counsel in a challenge to the truancy statute. The trial court held that because no right to counsel attached unless the possibility of detention arose, and because detention was not available at an initial truancy hearing, appointed counsel was not required at the initial truancy hearing. Appellants challenged that order and the contempt order. The court affirmed holding that because the initial truancy hearing was civil in nature, and because no significant liberty interest was at stake, appellants were not entitled to appointed counsel at the initial truancy hearing. The court also held that the trial court, via statute, was entitled to order punitive contempt for failure to comply with the order of truancy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order holding appellants were not entitled to counsel because the liberty interest at stake in the initial truancy petition hearing did not require the due process of appointed counsel. The court also affirmed the order holding appellants in contempt because such an order was consistent with the statute.

 

1316 of 2268 DOCUMENTS


State v. Donald


No. 21848-8-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 7



January 8, 1999, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Review Denied June 1, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Wash. LEXIS 394.
Reported in Table Case Format at: 93 Wn. App. 1048.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Pierce County. Docket No: 96-1-03101-3. Date filed: 03/18/97. Judge signing: Hon. Vicki L. Hogan.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his first degree robbery conviction from the Superior Court of Pierce County (Washington), arguing that the evidence was insufficient to prove that he was or pretended to be armed, that the lower court erred in admitting one of his pretrial statements, and that the Persistent Offender Accountability Act, Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.120(4), under which he received a life sentence without parole, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: In reviewing appellant's conviction for first degree robbery, the court found the evidence sufficient to allow a reasonable jury to find beyond a reasonable doubt that appellant obtained money by force or threat. Thus, the evidence was sufficient to support the jury's verdict. As to his pretrial statement, the court held that its admission was prejudicial, but where the lower court determined that it was relevant to show knowledge, intent, and preparation, even if its admission was an abuse of discretion, the error was harmless. In affirming appellant's sentence, the court determined that it was not disproportionate, that it was not cruel and unusual, and that appellant had failed to show a constitutional violation.


OUTCOME: The court found sufficient evidence to allow a jury to convict appellant of first degree robbery. The erroneous admission of appellant's pretrial statement as to prior bad acts was harmless and there was no constitutional violation in imposing a life sentence without parole under the Persistent Offender Accountability Act.

 

1317 of 2268 DOCUMENTS


State v. Marintorres


No. 21535-7-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


93 Wn. App. 442; 969 P.2d 501; 1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 10



January 8, 1999, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Jefferson County (Washington) that convicted him of first-degree escape and ordered him to pay the cost of a Spanish interpreter.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree escape. Fluent in Spanish, but not English, defendant claimed he left a prison work camp because he thought he had served his sentence. The trial court ruled that although defendant could testify to his personal understanding of the duration of his sentence, he could not relate what others had told him concerning his sentence. Defendant argued that his proposed testimony was not hearsay because it was relevant to his state of mind. The appellate court affirmed the conviction. Defendant's proposed testimony could fall within a hearsay exception if it was relevant to an issue at trial. An element of first-degree escape was that the inmate knew he was leaving confinement without permission. Defendant's testimony offered no evidence that he had permission to leave the camp. Thus, it was not relevant to a fact in issue and was inadmissible hearsay under Wash. R. Evid. 802. The appellate court, however, vacated an order requiring defendant to pay for a Spanish interpreter. The state violated notions of equal protection by requiring non-English speaking defendants to pay for an interpreter while not requiring other groups to pay for such service.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's first-degree escape conviction because he failed to show that he had permission to leave the prison work camp, and his proposed testimony was inadmissible as hearsay not within any exception and irrelevant. However, the court vacated the assessment of interpreter costs against defendant as a violation of equal protection.

 

1318 of 2268 DOCUMENTS



State v. Farr-Lenzini


No. 21969-7-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


93 Wn. App. 453; 970 P.2d 313; 1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 17



January 8, 1999, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant contested her conviction for attempting to elude a police officer under Wash. Rev. Code § 46.61.024 in the Superior Court of Clark County, (Washington), arguing that the arresting trooper's opinion testimony as to her state of mind violated her constitutional right to a jury trial under the federal and state constitutions.


OVERVIEW: In reviewing appellant's conviction for attempting to elude a police officer under Wash. Rev. Code § 46.61.024, the court held that because the officer's opinion testimony as to appellant's state of mind constituted harmful error, the conviction must be reversed. Further, it held that the traffic infraction of negligent driving in the second degree was not a lesser included offense of reckless driving. The record did not indicate that the trooper was qualified to testify as an expert on the driver's state of mind, so the court found an insufficient foundation to qualify the trooper as an expert for purposes of expressing an opinion as to appellant's state of mind. Further, the trooper's opinion was not admissible lay testimony. Thus, where the trooper testified to appellant's guilt without providing an adequate factual basis, the court concluded that the admission of this evidence was error. Because the trooper's testimony addressed the critical core issue as well as appellant's credibility, the court reversed and remanded.


OUTCOME: The court determined that the officer's opinion as to appellant's state of mind was harmful error, requiring reversal and remand, where the record did not show that the officer was qualified to testify as an expert on appellant's state of mind, and his opinion was not admissible as lay testimony where it was the province of the jury to decide appellant's guilt or innocence of the charge of attempting to elude a police officer.


 

1319 of 2268 DOCUMENTS


Pitzer v. Union Bank (in Re Estate of Magrini)


No. 21355-9-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


93 Wn. App. 421; 969 P.2d 113; 1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 1809



December 31, 1998, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, decedent's illegitimate children, sought review of an order by the Superior Court of Pierce County, Washington, which granted summary judgment to appellees, decedent's estate and the estate of decedent's widow, in appellant's suit to reopen decedent's estate and to impose a trust on assets in the estate of decedent's widow.


OVERVIEW: Appellants were illegitimate children of decedent father. After the death of decedent's widow, appellants learned the identity of their father and filed suit to reopen decedent's estate and impose a trust on his widow's estate. The trial court ruled against appellants. On appeal, the court reversed, and held that the personal representative of decedent's estate had a duty, if she knew appellants were decedent's children, to give them notice of the probate of his estate. Also, the court ruled that former Wash. Rev. Code § 11.04.080, which required that in order to inherit, illegitimate children had to have a written, signed acknowledgement of paternity by their father, was unconstitutional as a violation of equal protection. Because decedent's widow's failure to give appellants notice of probate created a jurisdictional defect in the final decree of distribution, appellants had grounds to reopen decedent's estate, and since the widow's estate contained the decedent's remaining assets, her estate was to be held in constructive trust for appellants.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court because the personal representative of decedent's estate had a duty to and failed to notify appellants, decedent's illegitimate children, of the probate of his estate, and therefore appellants could reopen the estate and impose a constructive trust on the widow's estate. The former statute requiring a signed statement of paternity in order for illegitimate children to inherit was unconstitutional.

State v. Davis


No. 15397-5-III


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION THREE, PANEL SEVEN


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 1805



December 29, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Review Denied June 1, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Wash. LEXIS 393.
Reported in Table Case Format at: 93 Wn. App. 1045.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Yakima County. Docket No: 95-1-01103-4. Date filed: 12/08/95. Judge signing: Hon. Michael Leavitt.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his convictions by the Superior Court, Yakima County, Washington, for taking a motor vehicle without permission and first degree robbery, for which he was sentenced to life imprisonment without parole as a habitual offender.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of taking a motor vehicle without permission and first degree robbery, and sentenced to life without parole under the Persistent Offender Accountability Act, Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.120(4). On appeal, the court affirmed, and held that § 9.94A.120(4) was constitutional, and that the Washington Supreme Court had already rejected the same challenges defendant raised. The court stated that the statute could, under appropriate circumstances, possibly constitute cruel and unusual punishment, but that in view of the seriousness of defendant's prior convictions, for first degree robbery and robbery with a deadly weapon, the life sentence was not cruel punishment. Defendant was not denied effective assistance of counsel because he could not show that better preparation by his attorney would have revealed bases for suppressing certain letters which were admitted against him. Evidence seized from a residence where defendant had been staying temporarily as a houseguest was properly admitted because the person who rented the premises validly consented to the search. Defendant's statements to police were admissible because they were voluntary.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and sentence to life imprisonment without parole as a habitual offender. There was sufficient evidence to support the convictions, and defendant's two prior convictions were the serious offenses of first degree robbery and robbery with the use of a deadly weapon, and thus, the sentence he received did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment.

Priorities First v. City of Spokane


No. 16872-7-III


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION THREE, PANEL EIGHT


93 Wn. App. 406; 968 P.2d 431; 1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 1779



December 22, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Review Denied June 1, 1999, Reported at: 137 Wn.2d 1035, 980 P.2d 1284, 1999 Wash. LEXIS 410.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Spokane County, Washington, denied appellant's, a political action committee, petition for writ of mandamus to have appellee city place a proposed initiative on the ballot. The trial court granted summary judgment to the city from which the committee appealed.


OVERVIEW: The issue before the appellate court was whether the initiative which required the city to obtain voter approval before it created provided off-street parking facilities exceeded the scope of the initiative power by encroaching on the functions the legislature had granted to the city council. The court held that the initiative interfered with the city council's authority under Wash. Rev. Code § 35.41 to create a special fund to defray the costs of a municipally owned facility. The political action committee relied on Wash. Rev. Code § 35.86 which granted authority to the city in its corporate capacity to provide and finance off-street parking, primarily for persons using park or civic center facilities. The court held that the proposed initiative applied outside the limited context of Wash. Rev. Code § 35.86 and conflicted with the authority granted in Wash. Rev. Code § 35.41. The trial court correctly determined that the initiative exceeded its proper scope when it required voter approval before the city could pledge or use on-street meter revenues or other city funds sources for off- street parking facilities.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

State v. Dahlquist


No. 41745-2-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 1752



December 14, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported in Table Case Format at: 93 Wn. App. 1034.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of King County. Docket No: 91-1-05957-4. Date filed: 10/30/97. Judge signing: Hon. Glenna S. Hall.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The defendant was convicted of third degree malicious mischief in the Superior Court of King County (Washington). He received a deferred sentence and was ordered to complete community service and pay financial obligations. After five years, the State moved to revoke his deferred sentence for wilful noncompliance. The court revoked his deferred sentence and the defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The defendant argued that the trial court abused its discretion by finding he willfully failed to pay his court ordered obligations. The trial court found that he willfully failed to pay because he continued to incur debt without paying toward his court-ordered financial obligations. Because he failed to make his bills part of the record, the court of appeals was unable to determine the nature of the debt. Nevertheless, he testified his monthly income was $ 1,400. He admitted he paid nothing toward his court costs or victim's assessment, despite having several years to pay. And he testified he owned a business and real property. Given that evidence, the trial court's finding of willful failure to pay was reasonable.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Hall

COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 1741

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Pierce County Superior Court (Washington), which convicted him of first degree burglary, two counts of second degree assault, and unlawful firearm possession.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that: (1) Initiative 159, the "Hard Time for Armed Crime Act," was unconstitutional because it violated the requirement that no bill shall embrace more than one subject; (2) the evidence was insufficient to convict him of assault; (3) the trial court erred by rejecting his proposed jury instruction that the intent to create a fear of injury in another may not be inferred from the mere display of the gun; (4) he was under the influence of medication and received ineffective assistance of counsel; and (5) the trial court erred by counting a prior Oregon robbery conviction in his offender score. The instant court rejected defendant's arguments, noting that the Washington Supreme Court already rejected defendant's position regarding the initiative's constitutionality. The jury could easily have found that defendant intended to create an apprehension of bodily harm in those persons within shooting range, and that two of the victims in particular had such an apprehension. The trial court properly refused defendant's proposed instruction, and defendant's Oregon robbery conviction was properly counted in his offender score.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Williams


No. 20933-1-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


93 Wn. App. 340; 968 P.2d 26; 1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 1677



December 4, 1998, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Superior Court of Pierce County (Washington) of unlawful manufacturing of marijuana and unlawful possession of marijuana. This appeal followed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed he was growing marijuana for his own medicinal use. Defendant's challenge to the constitutionality of the classification of marijuana was without merit. Defendant had no fundamental right to have marijuana as his preferred treatment. With respect to Schedule I drugs, there was not a defense of medical necessity. Regardless, defendant failed to show that no legal drug would have been as effective in treating his anxiety. Defendant failed to provide substantial evidence of medical necessity. The trial court did not err in refusing to give the medical necessity instruction. Because defendant failed to meet his evidentiary burden for the affirmative defense of medical necessity, the trial court's error in excluding expert testimony was not prejudicial.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Long


No. 21942-5-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 1694



December 4, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Review Denied May 5, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Wash. LEXIS 317.
Reported in Table Case Format at: 93 Wn. App. 1020.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Pierce County. Docket No: 96-1-00295-1. Date filed: 04/22/97. Judge signing: Hon. Frederick B. Hayes.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Superior Court of Pierce County (Washington) two counts of rape of a child in the first degree and two counts of molestation of a child in the first degree. This appeal followed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed the trial court erred in: (1) admitting expert opinions; (2) instructing the jury; and (3) permitting so much prejudicial evidence as to constitute cumulative error. Defendant opened the door to the State's expert's underlying theory of acting out sexually. The trial court did not err in admitting testimony concerning delayed reporting. One particular statement could have been understood as an opinion that defendant was guilty, but the untainted evidence was so overwhelming that the jury would have reached the same verdict even without the expert's opinion. Defendant also challenged a jury instruction which stated that the defendant's finger was considered an object. It was possible to read the instruction as suggesting that the trial court was of the opinion that the defendant had used his finger to commit the offense. Nonetheless, the error was harmless. The alleged errors did not satisfy the doctrine of cumulative error.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Williams


No. 40810-1-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 1654



November 23, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:  [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Snohomish County. Docket No: 96-1-01821-4. Date filed: 05/12/97. Judge signing: Hon. Ronald L. Castleberry.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Snohomish County, Washington, convicted defendant of possession of a depiction of a minor engaged in sexually explicit conduct in violation of Wash. Rev. Code § 9.68A.070. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's daughter had found seven to nine pictures of unclothed children in various poses interested in an adult magazine which was located in her father's dresser. He explained that he had such pictures because he was writing a series of articles on the availability of child pornography on the internet, and that he had hurriedly placed the pictures in the magazine when his daughter wanted to use his computer. Defendant later destroyed the pictures. Defendant argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred in denying his constitutional right to a unanimous jury verdict. The appellate court held that the failure to elect or request a unanimity instruction was an error because the prosecutor's mischaracterization of the photographs led the jury to believe that they were presented with sufficient evidence of sexually explicit pictures. Thus, the jury could have deliberated on the basis of different images, particularly, since they did not have the pictures available to them. Even though the State may have been correct that one sexually explicit picture supported a conviction, the court could not conclude that the jury agreed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed.

In re Detention of McCollum


No. 21686-8-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 1623



November 20, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Pierce County. Docket No: 95-2-12979-5. Date filed: 01/23/97. Judge signing: Hon. Karen L. Strombom.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed her commitment under Wash. Rev. Code § 71.09 in the Superior Court of Pierce County (Washington).


OVERVIEW: The appellate court held that, read as a whole, the record showed that the trial court barred evidence that defendant had a non-secure facility available to her. It did not bar evidence that defendant would not commit predatory acts if not confined in a secure facility. The record did not show that the trial court excluded relevant evidence, or that it refused to hear an offer of such evidence. The legislature acted constitutionally when it amended the definition of sexually violent predator. The state could not provide different procedural protections for those confined under the sex predator statute unless there was a valid reason. Equal protection required the state to comply with provisions of Wash Rev. Code § 71.05 as related to the consideration of less restrictive alternatives. The legislature was not required to exercise control over persons not likely to engage in predatory acts if not confined in a secure facility and the trial court was not required to consider evidence pertaining to the proper placement of such persons. The trial court did not address a claim of cruel and unusual punishment that had not been raised below.


OUTCOME: The decision of the lower court was affirmed.

Lund v. Department of Ecology


No. 21625-6-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


93 Wn. App. 329; 969 P.2d 1072; 1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 1575



November 13, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Order Granting Motion to Publish Opinion January 1, 1999.
Reported in Table Case Format at: 93 Wn. App. 1003.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff owner appealed the order of the Superior Court of Pierce County (Washington) denying him a conditional use permit to build a single-family residence over water.


OVERVIEW: The owner purchased the unimproved lot in 1978. In 1979, the Tacoma, Wash., Shoreline Master Program (TSMP) was enacted. In 1992, the owner applied for a conditional use permit to build a one-story pile-supported, single-family residence over the water. The Washington Department of Ecology denied the permit, finding that the proposed use did not conform with the TSMP. The trial and appellate courts affirmed. Tacoma, Wash., Shoreline Master Program §§ 13.10.175(B)(14)(1), 13.10.040(D), prohibited construction of residences over water. No policy overrode this prohibition. Thus, this prohibition was valid. Because there was no structure on the site when the TSMP was passed, the owner's use of the property was not pre-existing.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.

State v. Noga


No. 22166-7-II, No. 22186-1-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 1556



November 6, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported in Table Case Format at: 92 Wn. App. 1066.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Pacific County. Docket No: 97-1-00082-1. Date filed: 07/22/97. Judge signing: Hon. Joel M. Penoyar.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were charged with trafficking in stolen property, possession of stolen property, and forgery. Defendants moved to suppress and dismiss. The Superior Court of Pacific County, Washington, granted the motion and the case was dismissed. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court assumed that the arrests of defendants were valid. The court held that the arrests could justify the search of defendants' person, but nothing else. Accordingly, the search-incident exception was not significant with respect to the officers' activities throughout the house. The court found no emergency entitling the officer's to rely on the emergency exception; no evidence that a sweep would lead to other discovery; no evidence that seeing the envelope, box, or duffel bag in plain view would indicate evidence of a crime; and no facts related to inevitable discovery. The court also found that the application for a warrant to search defendants' house was based in large part on the items that the officers had unlawfully discovered. The warrant was fruit of the poisonous tree, and the motion to suppress was properly granted.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Jacobson


NO. 40100-9-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


92 Wn. App. 958; 965 P.2d 1140; 1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 1543



November 2, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Review Denied June 1, 1999, Reported at: 137 Wn.2d 1033, 980 P.2d 1282, 1999 Wash. LEXIS 379.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the exceptional sentence imposed by the Superior Court of King County (Washington) after he was convicted of vehicular homicide.


OVERVIEW: The superior court imposed an exceptional sentence upward finding defendant intentionally drove southbound in the northbound lanes of a divided highway at night with his headlights off while intoxicated and fleeing the police. On appeal, an exceptional sentence under Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.210(4)(a) will not be reversed unless the sentence was clearly erroneous or its stated reason did not justify an exceptional sentence as a matter of law. The court held defendant's exceptional and compelling distinguished conduct was more than the typical vehicular homicide case and that the severity and egregiousness of the facts fell between those of vehicular homicide and murder in the second degree. The court also held under Wash. Rev. Code §§ 9.94A.120(2), 9.94A.390(2)(e) the statutes from which the superior court derived the conduct more egregious than typical aggravating factor was not unconstitutionally vague as applied to defendant. The court held the void for vagueness doctrine applied to laws that attempted to "proscribe or prescribe conduct" and although sentencing statutes were not susceptible to vagueness attacks the discretionary elements of the statutes did not make them vague.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's exceptional sentence.

State v. Berry


NO. 40293-5-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 1525



October 26, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Review Granted May 6, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Wash. LEXIS 308.
Reported in Table Case Format at: 92 Wn. App. 1056.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of King County. Docket No: 95-1-04540-0. Date filed: 02/10/97. Judge signing: Hon. Patricia H. Aitken.



DISPOSITION: Conviction on count 1 reversed. Remainder of convictions and sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction by the Superior Court of King County (Washington) for four counts of first degree robbery.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was sentenced as a persistent offender. Defendant contended there was insufficient evidence to support his conviction on one of the robbery charges because the victim recognized that the gun he displayed was a BB pellet gun, not a firearm. Defendant argued that one of the prosecutor's peremptory challenges was purposefully racially discriminatory and that his right to equal protection was violated. Defendant raised several other arguments. The court determined there was insufficient evidence to support the conviction on one of the robbery charges because the victim knew the object displayed was not a firearm. The court denied defendant's request for discovery and hearing on the denial of his Batson challenge by the trial court. The court held the trial court had not erred by counting the stayed convictions as strikes because they would have been strikes in California. The court ascertained that defendant's sentence did not violate the constitutional prohibition against cruel and unusual punishment. The court affirmed all of the convictions but one and also affirmed the sentence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed all of the convictions but one because there was insufficient evidence.

State v. Adams


No. 39507-6-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 1457



October 19, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Review Denied May 5, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Wash. LEXIS 309.
Reported in Table Case Format at: 92 Wn. App. 1047.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of King County. Docket No: 96-1-02479-8. Date filed: 10/07/96. Judge signing: Hon. Linda Lau.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of King County (Washington), which convicted him of related charges of felony harassment, felony violation of a court order, stalking, residential burglary, and misdemeanor violation of a court order.


OVERVIEW: The charges were based on a series of incidents between defendant and the victim. The court affirmed the convictions. Defendant contended that he was denied effective assistance when his trial counsel failed to object to certain evidence. The court concluded that defendant failed to establish prejudice. An ambiguous reference that defendant had been in jail did not suggest a propensity to commit the charged offense. Similarly, the fact that defendant knew some drug dealers did not constitute a crime or a prior bad act. There was no reasonable likelihood that the outcome would have been different had the challenged testimony not been admitted. The court rejected defendant's arguments that the stalking statute, Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.46.110(1), was unconstitutional. Defendant contended that his due process rights to a fair trial were violated when the deputy prosecutor improperly attempted to impeach him with prior inconsistent statements during cross-examination. The court found that the questioning fell within the normal scope of cross-examination and was not subject to the rules governing the admission of prior inconsistent statements.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which convicted defendant of felony harassment, felony violation of a court order, stalking, residential burglary, and misdemeanor violation of a court order.

State v. Saucedo-Alvarez


No. 40570-5-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 1467



October 19, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported in Table Case Format at: 92 Wn. App. 1049.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of King County. Docket No: 96-1-05480-8. Date filed: 03/24/97. Judge signing: Hon. Larry Jordan.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision of the Superior Court of King County (Washington), which convicted him of two counts of child molestation in the first degree and one count of rape of a child in the first degree and ordered, as special conditions of community placement, that he have no contact with minor-age children, avoid places where minors were known to congregate, and refrain from possessing or perusing pornographic materials.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sexually molested the minor daughters of his live-in girlfriend. A jury found defendant guilty of first-degree child molestation and first-degree rape of a child. The trial court sentenced him to 172 months of confinement, followed by a period of community placement. In addition to the standard community placement conditions provided in Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.120(9), the court imposed several discretionary conditions. On appeal, the court affirmed defendant's convictions and sentence. The court held that the trial court properly refused to admit testimony by one daughter's ex-boyfriend concerning the daughter's reputation for truthfulness. The court stated that in light of the significant inconsistencies in the ex-boyfriend's testimony, the trial judge's decision was neither manifestly unreasonable nor lacking a tenable basis. The court also held that allowing a sex offender's community corrections officer or therapist to determine, on the basis of the offender's criminal history and sexual proclivities, whether a pornography restriction was necessary in a given case, was a reasonable, crime-related means of protecting the public from future acts of sexual violence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions of two counts of child molestation in the first degree and one count of rape of a child in the first degree. The court also affirmed defendant's sentence, including the special conditions of community placement.
