In re Detention of Broer


No. 37886-4-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


93 Wn. App. 852; 957 P.2d 281; 1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 920



June 15, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:

[As amended by order of the Court of Appeals March 4, 1999.]


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Order Denying Motion for Reconsideration and Amending Opinion March 4, 1999, Reported at: 973 P.2d 1074, 1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 454.
Amended by, Reconsideration denied by Broer v. State, 973 P.2d 1074, 1999 Wash. App. LEXIS 454 (Wash. Ct. App., 1999)
Review denied by Broer v. State (In re Broer), 138 Wn.2d 1014, 989 P.2d 1140, 1999 Wash. LEXIS 595 (1999)
Related proceeding at In re Det. of Broer, 126 Wn. App. 1060, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 1423 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prisoner sought review of a decision from the Superior court of King County (Washington) ordering prisoner to undergo a metal examination after respondent State of Washington commenced a civil commitment proceeding under the sexually violent predator act, Wash. Rev. Code §§ 71.09.010-.902. Prisoner also sought review of a contempt order for his refusal to be examined.


OVERVIEW: Several days before his scheduled release from prison, the state began a civil commitment proceeding against the prisoner under a sexually violent predator act. The state believed the prisoner met the statutory definition of a sexually violent predator under Wash. Rev. Code § 71.09.020(1) given his criminal history of sexual assaults against children. The prisoner challenged on jurisdictional grounds the order directing him to submit to a mental examination and the contempt order issued for refusing such examination. The court affirmed the trial court's order directing the prisoner's mental examination and the order holding him in contempt for refusing to be examined. The court ruled that the state's failure to make the proper allegations in its initial petition of the appropriateness of less restrictive alternatives to detention did not deprive the trial court of jurisdiction to issue the order for a mental examination. The error was considered harmless since less restrictive alternatives were considered at a probable cause hearing. The court found the trial court did not abuse its discretion in holding the prisoner in contempt for refusing to undergo the mental examination.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order directing the prisoner's mental examination under the sexually violent predator act and affirmed the order holding the prisoner in contempt for his refusal to be examined. The court concluded that the trial court order could not be collaterally attacked in contempt proceedings and held the court had inherent power to hold the prisoner in contempt.

 

1375 of 2268 DOCUMENTS


State v. Knowles


No. 20834-2-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


91 Wn. App. 367; 957 P.2d 797; 1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 912



June 12, 1998, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment entered by the Superior Court of Kitsap County (Washington), which convicted him of intimidating a judge under Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.72.160.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted under § 9A.72.160 of making threats as defined in Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.04.110(25)(d), (j). Defendant asserted that Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.72.160 was unconstitutionally overbroad and violated the free speech protections of the First Amendment. On appeal, the court affirmed defendant's conviction and held that § 9A.72.160 was not unconstitutionally overbroad. The definitions of "threat" in Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.04.110(25)(d), (j) included protected speech because they covered threats that were not "true threats" of bodily harm. Accordingly, the prohibited speech was entitled to some First Amendment protection. Because the speech in question was private rather than public speech, it could be regulated if the regulation was viewpoint neutral and reasonable in light of the purpose served. Relying on its recent decision analyzing the overbreadth doctrine relative to the public servant intimidation statute, Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.76.180, the court concluded that the judge intimidation statute was narrowly tailored to meet its compelling government interest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for intimidating a judge.

U.S. Oil & Ref. Co. v. Department of Revenue


No. 21674-4-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 1540



June 12, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Review Denied April 6, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Wash. LEXIS 259. As Amended by Order of the Appellate Court June 12, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 1500.
Reported in Table Case Format at: 91 Wn. App. 1026.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Thurston County. Docket No: 89-2-02923-2. Date filed: 01/31/97. Judge signing: Hon. Wm. T. McPhee.

This Opinion was originally published at: 1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 910.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant oil company challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Thurston County (Washington), which determined that the hazardous substance taxes and petroleum products taxes were excise taxes, not property taxes limited by Wash. Const. art. 7, § 1.


OVERVIEW: The oil company sought a refund of hazardous substances taxes and petroleum products taxes, contending that the trial court erred in determining that the taxes were not property taxes, and asserted that because they were property taxes, they violated the uniformity clause of Wash. Const. art. 7, § 1. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court noted that the obligation to pay an excise tax was based upon the voluntary action of the person taxed in performing the act, enjoying the privilege or engaging in the occupation which was the subject of the excise. The court explained that the element of absolute and unavoidable demand, as in the case of a property tax, was lacking. The court determined that liability for the taxes arose from exercising control over the substances from voluntarily acquiring or producing the taxed products, not from merely possessing the substances. The court explained that once the oil company had acquired the product and paid the tax, it could have possessed the products for as long as it wished without again being taxed. The court concluded that the taxes were excise taxes and not subject to the requirements of Wash. Const. art. 7, § 1.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which determined that the hazardous substance taxes and petroleum products taxes were excise taxes.

Conley v. Dick's Towing E.

COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION THREE, PANEL FIVE


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 813

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff truck owner appealed an order of the Superior Court of Douglas County (Washington) that dismissed his claims against defendant towing company and denied his motions for default judgment under Wash. Super. Ct. Civ. R. 55.


OVERVIEW: The owner left his truck at a mall due to a transmission problem. The towing company towed the truck at the mall's request. The owner paid the towing and impoundment fees including storage and applicable sales tax without protest and negotiated for continued vehicle storage. The towing company's clerical staff erroneously sent a notice of custody and sale for vehicle auction. The mistake was promptly discovered and no further action was taken. State v. Blackwell


No. 21842-9-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 770



May 22, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Kitsap County. Docket No: 96-8-01165-1. Date filed: 03/05/97. Judge signing: Hon. M. K. Haberly.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Kitsap County (Washington), which convicted him of obstructing a law enforcement officer in violation of Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.76.020.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, an African-American juvenile, was at the fairgrounds when a group of persons who were allegedly gang members was being escorted out. Defendant yelled profanities and accused a law enforcement officer of racism. The law enforcement officer directed defendant to leave. Defendant refused and was subsequently arrested and convicted of obstructing a law enforcement officer in violation of § 9A.76.020. Defendant appealed the conviction. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the evidence was sufficient to support defendant's conviction because the record indicated that defendant hindered the officer's efforts to restore peace by screaming obscenities and racial accusations at him and that defendant refused to obey repeated orders to leave the fairgrounds, which resulted in raising the tension level of a situation that the officer was attempting to diffuse peacefully. The court ruled that defendant's First Amendment rights were not violated because his arrest was not based on critical speech, but on his disruptive behavior toward the officer and his multiple refusals to leave the fairgrounds.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for obstructing a law enforcement officer.

State v. Alvarez


No. 37466-4-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 755



May 18, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Whatcom County. Docket No: 94-1-00496-7. Date filed: 08/17/95. Judge signing: Hon. Michael F. Moynihan.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision from the Superior Court of Whatcom County (Washington), which found him guilty of two counts of assault in the second degree with sexual motivation and sentenced him to life in prison without parole under the "three strikes and you're out" law pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.120(4).


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to assault in the second degree with sexual motivation and was sentenced as a habitual offender to life in prison without parole based on prior convictions for robbery and assault. He challenged his sentence and argued that the State was required to prove the alleged prior convictions beyond a reasonable doubt and that his prior robbery conviction was invalid. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the State was not required to prove the constitutional validity of his prior convictions unless they were previously declared unconstitutional or were facially unconstitutional. His assertion that he was not aware of the elements of his prior offense when he pled guilty did not render the conviction invalid on its face. The State properly proved that the prior convictions existed, and defendant failed to proffer any evidence to prove they were invalid. Thus, the trial court properly sentenced him to life without parole.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's sentence to life in prison without parole under the three strikes law.

 

1380 of 2268 DOCUMENTS


State v. Bridges


No. 35645-3-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


91 Wn. App. 102; 955 P.2d 833; 1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 761



May 18, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [***1] PUBLISHED IN PART


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of King County (Washington), which sentenced him to life imprisonment without the possibility of parole under the Persistent Offenders Accountability Act (POAA) following his conviction for second degree robbery.


OVERVIEW: Defendant qualified for sentencing as a persistent offender under the POAA because his second degree robbery conviction was a conviction for a felony that was a "most serious offense" under Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.030(21) and he had been convicted on two other occasions of such offenses. Defendant argued that the trial court erred by refusing to allow discovery or an evidentiary hearing to show that the decision to charge him as a persistent offender was motivated by racial bias and that the POAA violated constitutional due process guarantees. The court held that the trial court did not err when it refused to allow further discovery because defendant's evidence did not show that similarly situated defendants of other races could have been, but were not, prosecuted. The POAA did not violate substantive due process. Protecting society from criminals who repeatedly committed serious violent crimes was a compelling state interest, and determining which crimes to classify as serious was an appropriate legislative function. The POAA was drafted more narrowly than the habitual criminals statute, Wash. Rev. Code § 9.92.090, which had withstood a variety of constitutional challenges.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence.

State v. Tsui


No. 36079-5-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 702



May 11, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Snohomish County. Docket No: 94-1-00897-2. Date filed: 02/03/95. Judge signing: Hon. Richard J. Thorpe.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review from a judgment of the Superior Court of Snohomish County (Washington), which convicted defendant of three counts of delivery of cocaine in a case brought by the State of Washington.


OVERVIEW: An informant was arrested after she sold cocaine to a deputy. The informant entered into a deal with police and attempted to purchase cocaine from defendant on three occasions. Each time, the task force gave her money to buy cocaine and equipped her with a hidden electronic transmitter. The informant unexpectedly refused to testify at trial and the audiotape recordings were never admitted into evidence because they were unintelligible. Defendant contended that the evidence gathered through the use of the body wire was inadmissible under Washington's Privacy Act for lack of probable cause. However, defendant did not demonstrate that the issue was a matter of manifest constitutional error reviewable for the first time on appeal. Given the testimony at trial, it was reasonable for the trial court to have found that the proffered testimony of witnesses was cumulative and irrelevant. There was no abuse of discretion in excluding a witness testimony and in limiting defendant's testimony regarding his relationship with the informant. Drawing all reasonable inferences in favor of the State, the court concluded that there was ample evidence to sustain a conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, which convicted defendant of three counts of delivery of cocaine.

State v. Speed


NO. 39285-9-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 705



May 11, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Snohomish County. Docket No: 95-1-01956-5. Date filed: 08/02/96. Judge signing: Hon. Ronald L. Castleberry.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Snohomish County (Washington), which convicted him of four counts of committing second degree rape by engaging in sexual intercourse with a patient during a treatment session, in violation of Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.44.050(1)(d).


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a hypnotherapist and counselor, was convicted of second degree rape for engaging in sexual intercourse with patients during treatment sessions. On appeal, his conviction was affirmed. The court held that although the prosecutor had not disclosed any intent to use a tape in its rebuttal, the trial court acted within its discretion by admitting the tape and offering to continue the case. Defendant could not have been surprised by the tape as he made it himself and knew that it was in the prosecutor's hands. Defendant's speedy trial rights were also not implicated. The court also held that defendant did not establish his affirmative defense that his patients consented to have sex knowing that it was not therapeutic. Defendant was barred by the invited error doctrine from arguing that the affirmative defense of consent in Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.44.050(1)(d) violated due process of law by requiring him to negate an element of the crime because he proposed the jury instruction that gave him the burden of proving the affirmative defense. The argument was without merit in any event.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment convicting defendant of second degree rape.

 

1383 of 2268 DOCUMENTS


State v. Finlay


NO. 37605-5-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 706



May 11, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Review Denied January 8, 1999.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of King County. Docket No: 95-1-03017-0. Date filed: 11/06/95. Judge signing: Hon. Richard M. Ishikawa.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of King County (Washington), which convicted defendant of delivering cocaine and possession of cocaine with intent to deliver.


OVERVIEW: Following his conviction, defendant appealed arguing that his criminal prosecution, which followed a civil forfeiture proceeding under Wash. Rev. Code § 69.50.505, placed him twice in jeopardy of the law. Defendant also contended that his sentence for the intent to deliver conviction was an abuse of the trial court's discretion. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgments of conviction and sentence. The court found that under Wash. Const. art. I, § 9, proceedings under § 69.50.505 were not punishment to which jeopardy attached. Defendant was sentenced to 82 months' imprisonment. The court concluded that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in imposing defendant's sentence because the sentencing range was within statutory limits and was justifiably exceptional based upon the large quantity of cocaine found in defendant's possession. The fact that defendant had substantially more cocaine in his possession than would have been necessary for personal use was a legally acceptable exceptional sentence aggravating factor. Defendant's sentence did not violate his equal protection or his due process rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which convicted defendant of delivering cocaine and possession of cocaine with intent to deliver.

State v. Peters


No. 21021-5-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 666



May 1, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Pierce County. Docket No: 95-1-04149-5. Judgment or order under review. Date filed: 07/30/96. Judge signing: Hon. Vicki L. Hogan.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order from the Superior Court of Pierce County (Washington) that entered a jury conviction for first degree robbery.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of robbing a market after he confessed to the crime. Another individual also confessed to the crime, but the trial court denied defendant's motion to admit that individual's typed statement. The jury convicted him of first degree robbery, and on appeal, the court affirmed. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in refusing to admit the hearsay statement of confession because it was too vague to expose the individual to criminal liability and it did not exhibit the nine indicia of trustworthiness. The court also found no abuse of discretion by the trial court in denying defendant's requests for disclosure of an informant's identity or for an in-camera hearing on that issue because defendant was unable to show the relevance of the informant's tips. The trial court had denied defendant's motion to dismiss after the prosecutors failed to produce a police officer's field notes because defendant failed to show the materiality of those notes and failed to show that he was prejudiced by any misconduct. Defendant demonstrated neither prejudice nor denial of his right to fair trial. Thus, he was not entitled to a reversal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order that convicted defendant of first degree robbery.

 

1385 of 2268 DOCUMENTS


State v. Campbell


NO. 40101-7-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 658



April 27, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of King County. Docket No: 95-1-02988-1. Judgment or order under review. Date filed: 09/28/95. Judge signing: Hon. Robert Alsdorf.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a ruling from the Superior Court of King County (Washington), which assessed against defendant an enhanced sentence pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 69.50.435(a) after defendant was convicted of delivery of a controlled substance within 1,000 feet of the perimeter of a school bus route stop.


OVERVIEW: The bus stop at issue was unmarked and was used by only two schoolchildren; however, the stop was designated on a map as well as in a list of school bus route stops kept by the school district. The list was available to the public. Defendant urged that he was unaware of the presence of the stop and challenged the sentence enhancement. The court found that the facts were sufficient to support the sentence enhancement for delivery of a controlled substance within 1,000 feet of a school bus route stop because there were readily understandable and ascertainable means to determine the location of the stop. Had defendant called the school district, he would have been told that the school bus stop was located at the place in question. That there was a difference in interpretation of the map by two witnesses was immaterial because whatever difficulties there were in interpreting the map could have been cleared up by telephoning the office where the map was kept. The focus of the inquiry was not the number of children served by the bus stop or what kind of bus stopped there, but that the daily pick-up of students was sufficient to put defendant on notice that the stop was there.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the enhanced sentence that the trial court assessed against defendant for delivery of a controlled substance within 1,000 feet of a school bus route stop.

 

1386 of 2268 DOCUMENTS


State v. Lee


No. 39492-4-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 631



April 20, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Snohomish County. Docket No: 95-1-01024-0. Date filed: 09/23/96. Judge signing: Hon. Anita L. Farris.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Snohomish County (Washington) convicting him of second degree assault while armed with a deadly weapon and imposing an enhanced sentence based upon the use of a firearm. The State of Washington filed a notice of cross-appeal, which it later abandoned.


OVERVIEW: After a confrontation between two couples at an apartment complex, an assailant put a gun to the man's temple and hit him on the side of the head. Later, the man and woman were brought outside to view two men in a show-up procedure. Defendant was identified as the assailant, and he was charged with second degree assault. After a jury trial, defendant was convicted and an enhanced sentence was imposed based upon his use of a firearm. On appeal, the court affirmed conviction and sentence. The court ruled that defendant was not denied effective assistance of counsel due to his attorney's failure to file a motion to suppress the identification. The court held that defendant failed to prove that the identification procedure resulted in a substantial likelihood of misidentification or was impermissibly suggestive. Defendant also failed to show that the identifications were unreliable. Because a motion to suppress would likely have been unsuccessful, the court ruled that defendant failed to show that he was prejudiced by his attorney's failure to bring such a motion. The court also held that Initiative 159, under which the firearm enhancement was increased, was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court convicting defendant of second degree assault while armed with a deadly weapon and imposing an enhanced sentence based upon the use of a firearm.

 

1387 of 2268 DOCUMENTS


Western Homes v. City of Issaquah


No. 39169-1-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 638



April 20, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of King County. Docket No: 95-2-33158-1. Date filed: 07/12/96. Judge signing: Hon. Carol Schapira.



DISPOSITION: Reversed in part, affirmed in part, and matter remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant developer challenged the decision of the Superior Court of King County (Washington), which held that respondent city's bonus plan ordinance used to evaluate development density was constitutional. The city filed a cross-appeal on the trial court's judgment that the development commission lacked authority or jurisdiction to revoke the developer's variance and that the variance continued to apply.


OVERVIEW: The city's planning director found that the developer's revised site plan for a housing project was substantially similar to its predecessor's plan that was granted a variance and that subsequent revisions due to city review or regulation would qualify for the variance. The development commission later revoked the variance and reduced the site's bonus points that determined the development density. The developer appealed to the trial court, which held that the bonus plan ordinance was constitutional and that the development commission could not revoke the variance. The court affirmed that the variance was valid because the board of adjustment was the only entity with jurisdiction to grant or revoke a variance. The variance continued to apply because the changes in the proposed development were due to new ordinances and not the site itself. The court reversed the trial court's judgment that the bonus point award system was constitutional because: (1) the ordinance created vague and arbitrary standards for awarding higher densities; and (2) in its application, the review and awarding of bonus points was a largely subjective decision of the city and its development commission.


OUTCOME: The court found that the bonus plan ordinance was unconstitutional and reversed the trial court's judgment that held that it was constitutional. The court affirmed the trial court's decision that held that the developer's variance was valid and continued to apply. The court remanded the matter to the city council and ordered that further proceedings in the trial court awaited the final outcome of the approval process.

 

1388 of 2268 DOCUMENTS


Detention of Gaff


No. 36526-6-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


90 Wn. App. 834; 954 P.2d 943; 1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 641



April 20, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Review Denied December 4, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Wash. LEXIS 939.
Review pending at In re Detention of Gaff, 1998 Wash. LEXIS 862 (Wash., Nov. 12, 1998)
Review denied by In re Detention of Gaff, 1998 Wash. LEXIS 939 (Wash., Dec. 4, 1998)
Subsequent appeal at In re Det. of Gaff, 118 Wn. App. 1035, 2003 Wash. App. LEXIS 2561 (2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Snohomish County (Washington), which issued an order, in response to a sexually violent predator petition filed by the state under the Community Protection Act, committing him to a secure facility until he was safe to be at large.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had previously been convicted of several brutal assaults and rapes. He contended on appeal that prosecutorial misconduct required reversal, that his commitment constituted double jeopardy and violated substantive due process and ex post facto laws, and that the trial court erred by instructing the jury to consider the "feasibility" of treatments less restrictive than secure confinement. The court affirmed the judgment. The court noted that the impropriety in the prosecutor's arguments could readily have been corrected by objections and curative instructions, and determined that while the argument was improper, the court discerned no improper purpose and no likelihood that the jury's verdict reflected a desire to punish defendant rather than protect the public under the Community Protection Act. The court determined that the alleged futility of defendant's treatment was irrelevant to the question of punitive intent and did not amount to a double jeopardy or ex post facto violation. The court found that the invited error doctrine precluded review of the issue on the jury instruction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court ordering defendant to be civilly committed as a sexually violent predator.

 

1389 of 2268 DOCUMENTS


State v. Balzer


No. 21805-4-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


91 Wn. App. 44; 954 P.2d 931; 1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 594



April 17, 1998, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed the decision of Superior Court of Thurston County (Washington), which instructed the jury on defendant's affirmative defense that his possession and possession with intent to deliver marijuana were based upon religious free exercise under the Religious Freedom Restoration Act and that the state had to prove the absence of defendant's unwitting possession of cocaine beyond a reasonable doubt.


OVERVIEW: The state charged defendant with two counts of unlawful possession of a controlled substance, marijuana and cocaine, and one count of unlawful possession with intent to deliver. Defendant asserted two affirmative defenses: that he possessed marijuana for religious purposes under the Religious Freedom Restoration Act and that he was in unwitting possession of the cocaine. The court instructed the jury on both defenses and gave an instruction that the state had to prove the absence of the unwitting possession defense beyond a reasonable doubt. After an emergency interlocutory appeal, the court reversed, holding that Wash. Rev. Code § 69.50.401, which criminalized possession and distribution of marijuana even if for religious purposes, did not violate Wash. Const. art. I, § 11 because the state had a compelling interest in regulating marijuana, a dangerous drug. There were no less restrictive means for protecting the safety of citizens and an exception for specific religions would encourage enlistment in those religions for the wrong reasons. Defendant, not the state, bore the burden to prove the affirmative defense of unwitting possession by a preponderance of the evidence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's decision, holding that defendant was not entitled to an instruction regarding his religious freedom under the Religious Freedom Restoration Act because the regulation of marijuana, a harmful drug, was a compelling state interest with no less restrictive means of protecting the public. Defendant bore the burden to prove unwitting possession by a preponderance of the evidence.

State v. Ramseth


No. 15817-9-III, No. 15892-6-III


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION THREE, PANEL SEVEN


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 582



April 14, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Spokane County. Docket No: 95-1-02181-7. Date filed: 05/20/96. Judge signing: Hon. Kathleen O'Connor.



DISPOSITION: Convictions affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the Superior Court of Spokane County (Washington) determination that the police obtained a valid consent to search, the constitutionality of his sentences under the three strikes law, and certain trial issues. He sought a new trial after two separate convictions for first degree robbery and a sentence of life without parole under Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.120(4), the Persistent Offender Accountability Act (Act).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of three counts of first degree robbery in two separate jury trials presided over by different judges. He was sentenced for each conviction to life in prison without possibility of parole as a persistent offender, as he had two prior violent offenses. After he was arrested on suspicion of robbery, officers obtained consent to search an apartment where he was staying. One of the renters signed a consent card. Defendant's motion to suppress evidence obtained in that search was denied. He also unsuccessfully argued that the confrontation clause prohibited the consent card being introduced as evidence and that he should have been allowed to implicate a third party in the crimes. He appealed these issues and his sentencing as a persistent offender. The court noted that all of his challenges had been resolved previously by the state supreme court. The appellate court had no choice but to follow precedent. It found no error or abuse of discretion. The jury instructions were not vague or constitutionally deficient. The evidence supported the convictions in both cases. The judgments were affirmed.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions and persistent offender sentences were affirmed. There was no prejudicial error or abuse of discretion. The accumulation of errors principle was inapplicable.

State v. Sanchez


No. 14685-5-III


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION THREE,


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 583



April 14, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Review Denied September 30, 1998, Reported at: 1998 Wash. LEXIS 757.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Chelan County. Docket No: 94-8-00351-4. Date filed: 02/09/95. Judge signing: Hon. John E. Bridges.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed. Remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the Superior Court of Chelan County (Washington) juvenile court conviction of complicity to commit first degree murder, first degree burglary, attempted burglary, and theft of firearms, and a manifest injustice sentence, challenging admission of his confession, sufficiency of the evidence, and the exceptional sentence, and arguing that the theft of firearms and firearm enhancement statutes were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The 12-year-old defendant was convicted of the death of a man shot at least 18 times with guns stolen from a friend's home. The body and the boys were found on a riverbank after shots were heard, and the boys were read their rights and arrested. The defendant confessed when his mother told him to tell. The trial court imposed a sentence of 542 weeks, citing protection of the community and rehabilitation as compelling reasons to incarcerate him until age 21, and holding that a standard range sentence, which would have been 180 to 224 weeks, would effect a manifest injustice. The court noted that these factors supported a manifest injustice disposition and substantial evidence supported them. But absent facts documenting a need to confine him for a set duration to attend a specific treatment program, the assumption that he might be released early was too speculative to support the length of his sentence. It also found that independent circumstantial evidence supported a reasonable inference that he was involved in the murder and sufficiently established the corpus delicti to justify admitting his confession. The judgment was affirmed. The case was remanded for resentencing.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction was affirmed but the case was remanded for resentencing, specifically for recalculation of the sentence without regard to possible good time, which was too speculative. Defendant's argument that the firearm enhancements and theft of firearms charges must be dismissed because the omnibus violence prevention act was unconstitutional was without merit, as was his challenge to his confession.

State v. Paulinkonis

COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 575

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of his conviction for first degree murder from the Superior Court of King County (Washington).


OVERVIEW: At trial, appellant admitted killing his housemate, but told the jury that it was in self-defense. On appeal, appellant asserted that the to-convict instructions should have included the element of absence of self-defense, that the self-defense instruction given should have specified that self-defense was a defense for manslaughter as well as murder, that he was denied various constitutional rights, and that the trial court erred on several evidentiary rulings. State v. Davis


No. 38928-9-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


90 Wn. App. 776; 954 P.2d 325; 1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 578



April 13, 1998, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction in the Superior Court of King County (Washington) for assault. The State cross-appealed defendant's sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of burglary and assault. Defendant appealed his conviction, and the state cross-appealed, contending that the trial court erred by finding that the burglary and assaults were the same criminal conduct and by imposing a sentence for the burglary only. On appeal, the court affirmed the conviction. The sentencing was reversed and remanded for resentencing. There was sufficient evidence to prove first-degree burglary. Defendant was told to leave an apartment when he began yelling and pulled his gun. Thus, his license to enter the apartment was specifically revoked. The trial court erred in not imposing a sentence for the assault on a guest in the apartment. The assaults were on two different victims and as such could not be the same criminal conduct. The trial court did not abuse its discretion by refusing to punish each crime separately under the burglary antimerger statute. The trial court has discretion not to apply the burglary antimerger statute. The firearm enhancement under Initiative 159 was lawful because defendant was convicted of a crime in which he used a gun.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for assault. On the State's cross-appeal of defendant's sentence, the court reversed and remanded for resentencing.

State v. Murphy


NO. 40674-4-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 537



April 6, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of Snohomish County. Docket No: 96-1-01223-2. Date filed: 05/08/97. Judge signing: Hon. Thomas J. Wynne.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, citing the Sentencing Reform Act of 1981, Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.120(6), the Americans with Disabilities Act (ADA), 42 U.S.C.S. § 12101 et seq., and U.S. Const. amend. XIV, appealed a decision of the Superior Court of Snohomish County (Washington), which denied his request for the Drug Offender Sentencing Alternative (DOSA) and imposed a standard range sentence for possession of a controlled substance with intent to deliver.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that denial of the DOSA on the basis of his alleged disability violated the ADA and the Fourteenth Amendment. The court affirmed the decision. The court was not inclined to rule that the ADA applied in a sentencing context, but assuming that it did apply, the court stated that the relevant inquiry was whether defendant had established that he was a "qualified individual with a disability." The court determined that defendant had not proved his prima facie case. The court held that defendant's perceived impairment argument failed because he had not established that he was substantially limited in a major life activity. The court held that the record did not establish a prima facie case of disability discrimination under the ADA. The court held that denial of the DOSA in defendant's case withstood a substantive due process challenge because there was a rational basis for the sentencing court's decision. The court held that the goal of making frugal use of access to the DOSA treatment, through maximizing the effectiveness of the DOSA treatment program, comprised at least a substantial state interest, and that defendant's equal protection argument therefore failed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's imposition on defendant of a standard range sentence for possession of a controlled substance with intent to deliver.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Snohomish County (Washington), which based on a jury verdict convicted defendant of first degree assault.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court affirmed defendant's conviction of first degree assault. The court held that: (1) based on its de novo review of the evidence in this case the trial court correctly instructed the jury on the law of self-defense; (2) the trial court did not violate defendant's patient-physician privilege when it allowed his physician to testify as a rebuttal witness for respondent the state because defendant had waived the privilege by voluntarily testifying about his injuries; (3) the state was entitled to present evidence of defendant's "nonstatements," his failure to tell the police crucial exculpatory evidence at the time of his arrest, because he waived his right to remain silent and gave a statement to police; (4) although in violation of the trial court's limine order, the state's witnesses' references to the person with whom defendant had the altercation, as the victim, were not so prejudicial as having denied defendant a right to a fair trial; and (5) defendant's counsel provided effective assistance because defendant failed to show that his counsel's performance was both deficient and prejudicial.

State v. Parlor


No. 39931-4-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 470



March 30, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported in Table Case Format at: 1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 1926.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of King County. Docket No: 96-1-01244-7. Date filed: 12/09/96. Judge signing: Hon. Linda Lau.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court, King County (Washington) convicted defendant of unlawful possession of a firearm and possession of stolen property. On the last day of the trial, defendant failed to appear in court. Finding he was voluntarily absent, the court denied defendant's motion for a mistrial and allowed the trial to finish without him. Defendant appealed his convictions.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant contended that the trial court abused its discretion in electing to proceed with the trial in his absence. Defendant urged that the trial court, in deciding whether to proceed, should have applied the "complex of issues" test rather than the "voluntary waiver" test on the ground that the Washington Constitution provided greater protection for confrontation rights than did the Federal Constitution. The court affirmed. The court stated that although given the opportunity on several occasions to interpret the State confrontation guarantee more expansively than its Federal counterpart, no State of Washington court had done so. The court further found that the Hard Time for Armed Crime Act (Act), which increased the seriousness level and sentence for defendant's firearms conviction, was not unconstitutional. The court stated that penalty enhancement provisions in the Act were within the title of the Act, were constitutional, and were severable from allegedly unconstitutional provisions. The court further stated that the provision increasing the seriousness level of defendant's offense plainly fell within the subject of the title of the Act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for unlawful possession of a firearm and possession of stolen property.

State v. Washington


No. 38936-0-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 505



March 30, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.

DISPOSITION: Remanded with instructions to strike the "obey all laws" condition in O'Neill's sentence and to hold a new restitution hearing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court, King County (Washington), after accepting defendant's guilty plea to first degree robbery, imposed a sentence that included restitution, a term of community placement including requirements that defendant "obey all laws" and make reasonable progress in the department of correction's Moral Reconation Therapy (MRT) program. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant contended that the trial court lacked statutory authority to impose MRT as a condition of community placement and that the trial court exceeded its authority in requiring her to "obey all laws" while on community placement. The court remanded with instructions to strike the "obey all laws" condition and to hold a new restitution hearing. The court stated that because evidence about the nature of MRT was not properly before it because it was not presented to the trial court and defendant failed to supplement the record on appeal, it could not say that the trial court exceeded its authority in imposing the MRT condition. The court accepted the State's concession that the trial court erred in requiring defendant to "obey all laws" while on community placement. The court further stated that the trial court's restitution order must be vacated and the matter remanded for a new hearing because neither defendant nor her counsel were present at the restitution hearing.


OUTCOME: The court remanded with instructions to strike the "obey all laws" condition in defendant's sentence and to hold a new restitution hearing.

State v. Goodall

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of King County. Docket No: 96-1-00841-5. Date filed: 06/10/96. Judge signing: Hon. Marilyn R. Sellers.



DISPOSITION: Convictions affirmed but remanded to correct the scrivener's error in Goodall's judgment and sentence.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of King County (Washington) that convicted him, following a jury trial, of first degree unlawful possession of a firearm under Wash. Rev. Code § 9.41.040(1)(a) and of possessing a stolen firearm under Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.56.310. His convictions followed the passage of the Hard Time for Armed Crime Act (Act).


OVERVIEW: Defendant alleged five bases for reversal in his appeal from his convictions. The court rejected each of them and affirmed the convictions. The court held that: (1) the provisions of the Act were severable, (2) § 13 of the Act did not fall outside the scope of the Act's restrictive legislative title and therefore did not violate Wash. Const. art. II, § 19, (3) a juvenile adjudication constituted a conviction for purposes of the 1995 unlawful possession statute, (4) defendant failed to show ineffective assistance of counsel, and (5) defendant's pre-Miranda statement was not the product of custodial interrogation. The court noted a clerical error in defendant's judgment and sentence. Instead of stating that he was convicted of possessing a stolen firearm under Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.56.310, the record erroneously stated that defendant was convicted of second degree possession of stolen property under Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.56.160. The case was remanded for correction of the scrivener's error.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for possessing a stolen firearm and first degree unlawful possession of a firearm. The court remanded the case to correct the scrivener's error in defendant's judgment and sentence.

 

1399 of 2268 DOCUMENTS


State v. Brown


No. 38476-7-I


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION ONE


1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 511



March 30, 1998, Filed


NOTICE:   [*1] RULES OF THE WASHINGTON COURT OF APPEALS MAY LIMIT CITATION TO UNPUBLISHED OPINIONS. PLEASE REFER TO THE WASHINGTON RULES OF COURT.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported in Table Case Format at: 1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 1882.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of King County. Docket No: 95-1-06313-2. Date filed: 04/15/96. Judge signing: Hon. Patricia H. Aitken.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of King County (Washington) in favor of the State of Washington, which convicted him of unlawful possession of a firearm after a jury verdict. The trial judge had permitted a police officer to testify that his decision not to fingerprint the gun found in the car defendant was driving was based on unspecified information he received from defendant at the scene.


OVERVIEW: The trial judge had previously suppressed statements defendant had made to the officer, before having been read his Miranda rights, that the gun was not his and that he was just delivering it for a friend. However, the judge determined that defendant had "opened the door" by inquiring about whether the gun had been fingerprinted. After the testimony, the judge immediately gave a limiting instruction on its use. During deliberations, the jury sent a note asking about the instruction and the officer's testimony. The judge decided not to reinstruct the jury. On appeal, the court held that it was not error to permit the officer to testify, because his testimony did not substantially describe defendant's suppressed statements, defendant had "opened the door," and the limiting instruction had been sufficient. The court held that a reinstruction had not been required because the jury's note had not signaled any erroneous understanding or misapplication of the law. The court also held that the penalty enhancement provisions of the Hard Time for Armed Crime Act, 1995 Wash. Laws, ch. 129, Initiative 159, were not unconstitutional because they plainly fell within the subject of the Act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the superior court's judgment in favor of the State, which had convicted defendant of unlawful possession of a firearm.

 

1400 of 2268 DOCUMENTS


Holland v. City of Tacoma


No. 21522-5-II


COURT OF APPEALS OF WASHINGTON, DIVISION TWO


90 Wn. App. 533; 954 P.2d 290; 1998 Wash. App. LEXIS 443



March 27, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for Review Denied September 30, 1998, Reported at: 136 Wn.2d 1015, 966 P.2d 1278, 1998 Wash. LEXIS 770. As Corrected May 22, 1998.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed from the Superior Court of Pierce County (Washington), which entered summary judgment dismissing his lawsuit challenging the constitutionality of a City of Tacoma ordinance that limited the volume of sound projected from car sound systems. Plaintiff also appealed the award of attorney fees to the city.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was convicted of violating a city ordinance that prohibited the playing of car sound systems at a volume that would be audible at a distance greater than 50 feet. After the conviction against him was dismissed with prejudice, plaintiff brought this action challenging the constitutionality of the ordinance. The lower court dismissed the case as frivolous and awarded attorney's fees to the city. Plaintiff appealed and the court affirmed the dismissal but reversed the award of attorney's fees. Plaintiff's first amendment free speech claims failed because he admitted that he was not attempting to communicate a message, he was merely listening. This was pure conduct, not speech so the first amendment claim failed. The vagueness claim failed because the standard applied by the ordinance, audibility, was not subjective, and the ordinance supplied clear guidelines, audibility at 50 feet, which were understandable by people of ordinary intelligence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of plaintiff's action challenging the constitutionality of the city ordinance, but reversed the award of attorney fees.
